# Validity of Results

The general agreement between the calculated and observed effluent partial pressures of HF and $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , in view of the variety of experimental conditions, supports the method of measurement and indicates that the equilibria assumed were correct and sufficient. Some specific examples which support these equilibria are given below.

# Saturated Melts

The first clearcut indication of the formation of hydroxide in measurable amounts came from the variation of effluent pressures with respect to volume of carrier gas. When a mixture of HF and $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}$ was introduced into a BeO-saturated melt containing little or no hydroxide, the effluent partial pressures of HF and $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}$ (x and y) increased with the increase in volume of gas passed in such a manner that $x^{2}/y$ remained constant, while x/y decreased. Since $Q_{0} = x^{2}r/y$ and $Q_{A} = xs/y$ , the quantity $x^{2}/y$ would be constant when r is constant (fixed by BeO saturation) and x/y would decrease as s increases.

The influent partial pressures of HF and $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}$ were varied and the resulting Q's compared. Runs 101, 103, and 123 for 0.316 BeF $_2$ at $700^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ (with reported Q values of 2.18 x $10^{-2}$ , 2.47 x $10^{-2}$ , and 2.13 x $10^{-2}$ , respectively) had influent pressures such that influent $(\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{HF}})^{2} / (\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}})$ were 4.36 x $10^{-2}$ , 4.46 x $10^{-2}$ , and 4.25 x $10^{-3}$ , respectively. Runs 105, 107, 109, and 111 for 0.316 BeF $_2$ at $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ (with reported Q values of 4.61 x $10^{-3}$ , 4.07 x $10^{-3}$ , 4.09 x $10^{-3}$ , and 4.69 x $10^{-3}$ , respectively) also indicated that the observed Q's were independent of the influent pressures, with influent $(\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{HF}})^{2} / (\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}})$ values being 6.52 x $10^{-2}$ , 2.28 x $10^{-2}$ , 5.54 x $10^{-3}$ , and 4.98 x $10^{-3}$ , respectively.

These runs include cases where the influent and effluent pressures were nearly identical and also where quite a bit of HF was required to react with oxide to form water and the reverse (H₂O reacting with fluoride to form HF). All three gave Q's in agreement. In the early runs, the flow rate of carrier gas was varied from 50 to 150 ml/min and no variation in the effluent pressures was observed.

The $Q_A$ values determined using saturated melts were similarly found to be independent of the ratios of HF and $H_2O$ in the influent stream. Also, $Q_A$ was shown to be independent of whether hydroxide was being added or removed by the gas stream. For example, see the odd-even pairs 401-402, 405-406, and 407-408 in Table 3 for 0.300 BeF $_2$ at 652, 601 and $700^\circ C$ , respectively.

The proton balance between influent and effluent gas streams, after the initial region where hydroxide was being formed, also attests to the validity of the measurement.

After completion of the 200 series the melt was transferred from the vessel through a sintered nickel filter and the material on the filter examined by x-ray diffraction. The characteristic peaks for LiF-BeF $_2$ and BeO were observed. There was no indication of compound formation between BeO and the fluoride melt or of other constituents [such as Li $_2$ O or Be(OH) $_2$ ]. Unsaturated Melts

In addition to the tests performed with saturated melts, the validity of $Q_0$ was checked by conducting experiments in which oxide was being added and also when oxide was being removed. The former, more precise results, and the latter were in reasonable agreement.

The total pressure of the reactive gases was varied by changing the temperature of the saturator with no noticeable effect on the $Q_{0}$ and $Q_{A}$ values.

# IV. DISCUSSION

The measured equilibrium quotients, $Q$ , $Q_A$ , and $Q_O$ , were correlated with temperature and melt composition in terms of simple algebraic expressions. In the case of $Q$ , wherein $K_a = Q / a_{BeF_2}$ , the resulting correlation can be used to derive a considerable amount of thermodynamic information about the LiF-BeF $_2$ solutions and, by extrapolation, about pure BeF $_2$ liquid. Combination of $Q$ and $Q_O$ can be used to obtain the concentration of oxide at BeO saturation. The variation of $Q_A$ with melt composition and temperature reflects the stability of hydroxide in LiF-BeF $_2$ melts.

# Correlation of Q

For each composition, the Q values were correlated according to the equation $\log Q = \text{slope}(1 / T) + \text{constant}$ . The Q's were weighted by an amount inversely proportional to their variance since all measurements did not follow the same parent distribution. The values of the parameters are presented in Table 6.

Since one of the primary objectives was determination of thermodynamic properties of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ , extrapolation of these data as a function of $X_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ to the pure liquid $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ was desirable. For a given temperature,

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}}\right) \text {v s} (\mathrm {X} _ {\text {L i F}}) ^ {2}
$$

forms a parabola for the composition range 0.30 to 0.80 $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ , thus the Q values at various compositions were correlated by the function

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = \mathrm {k} + 1 \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\text {L i F}}\right) ^ {2} + \mathrm {m} \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\text {L i F}}\right) ^ {4}.
$$

From definition in Chapter I, $\log K_{a} = k$ , therefore, correlation of $k$ as a function of $l / T$ will yield the thermodynamics of the desired reaction. Both $l$ and $m$ also show linear dependence upon $l / T$ . For the coefficients

expressed according to $\mathbf{k} = \mathbf{k}^{\circ} + \mathbf{k}^{\prime}(1 / \mathbf{T})$ , etc.

$$
\begin{array}{l} k ^ {0} = 3. 9 0 0 \pm 0. 0 1 9 k ^ {\prime} = - 4 4 1 8 \pm 1 7 \\ 1 ^ {\circ} = 7. 8 1 9 \pm 0. 2 2 5 \quad 1 ^ {\prime} = - 5 4 4 0 \pm 2 1 8 \\ m ^ {0} = - 1 2. 6 6 \pm 0. 6 0 m ^ {\prime} = 5 2 6 2 \pm 5 1 3 \\ \end{array}
$$

From the above parameters, equations were generated for each experimental composition as a function of $(1 / T)$ . The smoothed parameters obtained are tabulated in Table 7. The lines generated by the smoothed parameters for the various experimental compositions are shown in Figure 18 along with $\log K_{a}$ vs $(1 / T)$ for pure $\mathrm{BeF}_2(1)$ based on the extrapolation. Figure 19 provides a measure of this correlation. The quantity $Q_{obs} / Q_{calc}$ is shown for each experimental run. The standard deviation for each point is represented by a vertical bar.

Activity of $\mathbf{BeF}_2$ and LiF

Since $Q$ is equal to $\mathrm{K}_{\mathrm{a}}(\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{BeF}_2})$

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = \log \mathrm {K} _ {\mathrm {a}} \left(\mathrm {a} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) / \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = \log \mathrm {K} _ {\mathrm {a}} + \log \left(\mathrm {a} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} / \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right).
$$

By definition, $a_{\text{BeF}_2}$ is unity for pure liquid $\text{BeF}_2$ ( $X_{\text{BeF}_2} = 1$ ), therefore, $\log (a_{\text{BeF}_2} / 1) = 0$ . Since $X_{\text{LiF}} = 0$ at this point,

$$
\log K _ {a} = k ^ {o} + k ^ {\prime} (1 / T) \cdot
$$

In order to solve for $\log (a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} / x_{\mathrm{BeF}_2})$ which is $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ , at any composition the value of $\log K_{a}$ must be subtracted from $\log (Q / X_{\mathrm{BeF}_2})$ leaving $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} = [1^{\circ} + 1' (1 / T)]X_{\mathrm{LiF}}^{2} + [m^{\circ} + m'(1 / T)]X_{\mathrm{LiF}}^{4}$ .

At a specified temperature, the variation in $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ with composition is given by

$$
\log \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = \alpha \mathrm {X} ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + \beta \mathrm {X} ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}}.
$$

Values for the parameters at three temperatures are presented below:

<table><tr><td>Temp (°C)</td><td colspan="2">α ± σ</td><td colspan="2">β ± σ</td></tr><tr><td>500</td><td colspan="2">0.783 ± 0.380</td><td colspan="2">-5.85 ± 0.89</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>1.589</td><td>0.356</td><td>-6.66</td><td>0.85</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>2.229</td><td>0.338</td><td>-7.25</td><td>0.80</td></tr></table>

The quoted uncertainties in $\alpha$ and $\beta$ lead to an average uncertainty of $\pm 0.10$ units in $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ . From these uncertainties the values $100^{\circ}$ apart would overlap within the quoted uncertainty, but the values for 500 and $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ definitely do not overlap.

According to the Gibbs-Duhem Equation, if the activity (or activity coefficient) of one component is known as a function of composition, the other can be determined by integration of the equation $d \ln (\gamma_2) = -X_1 / X_2 \, d \ln (\gamma_1)$ .

From the equations given for $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ as a function of composition at specified temperatures, integration was carried out as follows:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = c x ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + \beta x ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} \\ \mathrm {d} \log \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = (2 \alpha \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 4 \beta \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} ^ {3}) \mathrm {d X} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} \\ \mathrm {d} \ln \gamma_ {\mathrm {L i F}} = - \left[ \frac {(1 - x _ {\mathrm {L i F}})}{x _ {\mathrm {L i F}}} \right] (2. 3 0 3) \left(2 \alpha x _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 4 \beta x _ {\mathrm {L i F}} ^ {3}\right) d x _ {\mathrm {L i F}} \\ \ln \gamma_ {\mathrm {L i F}} = (2. 3 0 3) \int (- 2 \alpha + 2 \alpha x _ {\mathrm {L i F}} - 4 \beta x ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 4 \beta x ^ {3} _ {\mathrm {L i F}}) d x _ {\mathrm {L i F}} \\ \log \gamma_ {\mathrm {L i F}} = - 2 \alpha x _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + \alpha x ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} - (4 / 3) \beta x ^ {3} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + \beta x ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + C. \\ \end{array}
$$

Two methods for evaluating C for various temperatures were considered. For a given temperature, if $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ is known for one composition, C can be determined and, hence, $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ determined for all compositions. First,

$\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ was calculated from the reported composition at LiF liquidus at various temperatures where $a_{\mathrm{LiF}} \equiv 1$ and $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}} \equiv 1 / X_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ . The values for $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ at the various temperatures were determined from the published phase diagram which was presented in Chapter I. The second method involved the use of the Q values for BeO-saturated melts in contact with solid LiF to determine the $X_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ at saturation. (The measured Q's are tabulated in Table 3 and the parameters for smoothed fit are given in Table 6.) The equations for $\log a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ as a function of composition and $\log a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ at LiF saturation were solved simultaneously to obtain $X_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ . With the concentration of LiF at $a_{\mathrm{LiF}} = 1$ known, $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ could be evaluated. The following values for C were obtained by the two methods.

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Temp (°C)</td><td colspan="3">From Thoma&#x27;s Phase Diagram</td><td colspan="3">From Solution of Equations</td></tr><tr><td>XBeF2</td><td>γLiF</td><td>C</td><td>XBeF2</td><td>γLiF</td><td>C</td></tr><tr><td>500</td><td>0.329</td><td>1.490</td><td>-0.299</td><td>0.321</td><td>1.473</td><td>-0.328</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>0.280</td><td>1.389</td><td>+0.083</td><td>0.281</td><td>1.391</td><td>+0.086</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>0.208</td><td>1.263</td><td>+0.283</td><td>0.256</td><td>1.344</td><td>+0.451</td></tr></table>

Agreement of values of C determined by both methods is very good at 500 and 600 but not at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Although LiF saturation at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ occurs at 0.208 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , the value of C corresponding to a calculated saturation value of 0.256 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ was considered more acceptable because the parabolic function does not fit the data well below 0.30 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . In fact, at 0.25 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ (data at 650 and $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) the fit is very poor.

Other empirical expressions were tried in an attempt to improve the fit in this region. No improvement was obtained using expressions containing additional odd-powers of $\mathbf{X}_{\mathbf{LiF}}$ , but a very good fit was obtained from 0.25 to 0.50 $\mathbf{BeF}_2$ by using an equation containing $\mathbf{X}^2$ , $\mathbf{X}^4$ , and $\mathbf{X}^6$

terms. Correlation of the data at $700^{\circ}C$ gave the following equation:

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = - 0. 7 2 3 + 4. 0 9 4 \mathrm {X} ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} - 1 6. 6 4 \mathrm {X} ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 1 2. 5 1 \mathrm {X} ^ {6} _ {\mathrm {L i F}}
$$

This equation should be compared with the correlation using only $\mathbf{X}^2$ and $\mathbf{X}^4$ terms:

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = - 0. 6 4 0 + 2. 2 2 9 \mathrm {X} ^ {2} _ {\text {L i F}} - 7. 2 5 \mathrm {X} ^ {4} _ {\text {L i F}}
$$

Both fit equally well between 0.33 and 0.50 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , but the former fits better for concentrations below 0.33 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ and the latter fits better for concentrations above 0.50 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . In order to obtain values for $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ from the former equation, the constant term was adjusted to yield the same value of $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ (at 0.40 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ ) as was given by the simpler expression used to express activities above 0.30 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . The expression obtained for $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ was given by:

$$
\log \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = - 0. 0 3 8 2 + 4. 0 9 4 \mathrm {X} ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} - 1 6. 6 4 \mathrm {X} ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 1 2. 5 1 \mathrm {X} ^ {6} _ {\mathrm {L i F}}
$$

The Gibbs-Duhem integration was carried out on this equation in order to obtain the expression for log $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \gamma_ {\mathrm {L i F}} = - 8. 1 8 8 \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 4. 0 9 4 \mathrm {X} ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + 2 2. 2 0 \mathrm {X} ^ {3} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} - 1 6. 6 4 \mathrm {X} ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} - 1 5. 0 1 \mathrm {X} ^ {5} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} \\ + 1 2. 5 1 \mathrm {X} _ {\text {I i F}} ^ {6} + \mathrm {C}. \\ \end{array}
$$

If this equation is solved simultaneously with the equation for $\log a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ at LiF saturation, the concentration equals 0.208 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ (the same as reported by Thoma) and the value of C equals 1.124.

Figure 20 shows a plot of $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ and $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ as a function of composition at 500, 600, and $700^{\circ}C$ . The dashed lines indicate the values at $700^{\circ}C$ (below 0.30 $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ ) when the equation with the $X^{6}_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ term was used. (The overall fit was not improved by using the extra term, hence the simpler form was used for the more general correlation.) The dotted line indicates the values of $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ at which the solid LiF phase would appear

and the values of log $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ at which the pure liquid $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ phase would appear.

The $(\cdots)$ line shows the predicted behavior of $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ on the basis of a simple model in which, for the region 0.33 - 1.0 BeF2, the components are redefined as BeF2 and Li2BeF4 and the activity of BeF2 is assumed equal to the mole fraction X'BeF2. The value of X'BeF2 is related to X BeF2, the mole fraction in LiF-BeF2 mixtures, by

$$
X ^ {\prime} _ {B e F _ {2}} = \left(3 X _ {B e F _ {2}} - 1\right) / 2 X _ {B e F _ {2}}.
$$

Since $a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} = X'_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ as defined above, the activity coefficient of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ in LiF-BeF2 mixtures is given by

$$
\gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = \left(3 \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - 1\right) / 2 \mathrm {X} ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}.
$$

This procedure of redefining components is similar to the method of Flood and Urnes; however, the assumed proportionality of $a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ to its mole fraction is purely arbitrary and the resulting correlation, which is rather good, is, therefore, entirely empirical. It might be more reasonable in this model to expect $a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ to be proportional to the product of the cation fraction of $\mathrm{Be}^{2+}$ and the anion fraction of $(\mathbf{F}^{-})$ squared [i.e., $a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ proportional to $(\mathbf{X}'_{\mathrm{BeF}_2})^3$ ], the melt being considered to be a simple mixture of the four ions $\mathrm{Li}^{+}$ , $\mathrm{Be}^{2+}$ , $\mathbf{F}^{-}$ , and $\mathrm{BeF}_4^{2-}$ . In order to rationalize the proportionality of $a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ to $\mathbf{X}'_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ it seems necessary to adopt one of the following unattractive models for the components $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ (undissociated) + $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ (undissociated) or

$$
I i _ {2} ^ {2 +} B e F _ {4} ^ {2 -} + B e ^ {2 +} B e F _ {4} ^ {2 -}.
$$

A more realistic approach to an interpretation of the behavior of $a_{\text{BeF}_2}$ and $a_{\text{LiF}}$ can perhaps be made in terms of models such as have been

proposed by Førland. $^{51}$ According to this model $\mathrm{BeF}_2(1)$ is assumed to be a three-dimensional polymer of $\mathrm{BeF}_4^{2-}$ tetrahedra with common corners, and the addition of LiF is assumed to break the Be-F-Be bonds. However, no such correlation will be attempted here.

Two features of the observed variation of $a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ are especially noteworthy:

(1) The rapid drop in $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ as $\mathbf{X}_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ approaches 0.33, at all temperatures, suggests the formation of $\mathrm{BeF_4}^{2 - }$ ion.   
(2) The temperature coefficient of $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ indicates increasing positive deviations from Raoult's law with increasing temperature at high $\mathbf{X}_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ values.

The activities of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ and LiF were calculated from the activity coefficients as a function of composition and temperature. The activities at 500, 600, and $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ are shown in Figure 21. The plot of activities, which should be proportional to the partial pressures of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ and LiF, corresponds to the predicted behavior of a system showing incomplete miscibility.[52] Since the positive deviations increase with temperature, these data seem to indicate that the system has a lower consolute temperature near $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and 0.80 $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ . Although the number of determinations of Q in this area is not extensive enough to definitely show the formation of two liquid phases, the indication is strong enough to warrant further investigation of this possibility. To date there is no clearcut evidence of partial immiscibility in the LiF-BeF $_2$ system although various investigators have considered the possibility of such occurrence.[53-55] The MgO-SiO $_2$ system, which has frequently been compared with the LiF-BeF $_2$ system,[1] indeed does show a miscibility gap. At least one case has been reported

for a similar fluoride melt. At low LiF concentrations in the system, $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ , evidence was obtained from quenched samples that two immiscible liquids were formed above the $\mathrm{ZrF_4}$ primary phase field.[56]

The LiF activity exhibits large positive deviations at low $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ concentrations; however, it should be kept in mind that these activities are referred to the solid rather than the supercooled liquid (if the latter were used as the reference state, all of the activities would be lower). The activities reported in the present work are in general agreement with previous measurements (summarized in Table 2). The largest discrepancy is in the value for LiF at 0.50 $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ reported by Berkowitz and Chupka.[22] The value for LiF by Buchler[24] is in reasonable agreement. Also, Buchler obtained greater activities for $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ at higher temperatures.[23] Special attention should be given to the value reported for 0.67 $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ (displaying very large positive deviations) which corresponds very closely to present values. This agreement is particularly gratifying since three completely different techniques have been used to obtain the values. Also, the previous studies were performed with melts which did not have BeO present. The presence of BeO would not be expected to affect the activities since BeO is only slightly soluble. Beryllium oxide could not have affected the Gibbs-Duhem integration since BeO was always present as a saturating phase, thereby restricting its activity to unity.

The expression for the relative partial molal heat content is given by:

$$
\frac {\partial \ln \gamma_ {1}}{\partial T} = - \frac {\left(\overline {{H}} _ {1} - H _ {1} ^ {0}\right)}{R T ^ {2}}.
$$

From the expression for $\log \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ , the appropriate differentiation may

be carried out to obtain

$$
\frac {\partial \log \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}}{\partial \mathrm {T}} = - \left(1 ^ {\prime} X _ {\mathrm {L i F}} ^ {2} + m ^ {\prime} X _ {\mathrm {L i F}} ^ {4}\right) (1 / \mathrm {T}) ^ {2}.
$$

Therefore,

$$
\left(1 ^ {\prime} X _ {L i F} ^ {2} + m ^ {\prime} X _ {L i F} ^ {4}\right) = \left(\bar {H} _ {B e F _ {2}} - H _ {B e F _ {2}} ^ {0}\right) / 2. 3 0 3 R
$$

and

$$
\left(\bar {\mathrm {H}} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - \mathrm {H} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {\circ}\right) = 2. 3 0 3 \mathrm {R} \left[ (- 5 4 4 0 \pm 2 1 8) \mathrm {x} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} ^ {2} + (5 2 6 2 \pm 5 1 3) \mathrm {x} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} ^ {4} \right]
$$

Values for the relative partial molal heat contents at various compositions are tabulated below:

$$
\begin{array}{l l l l} \frac {\mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}}{0 . 8 0} & \frac {\left(\overline {{\mathrm {H}}} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - \mathrm {H} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {\circ}\right)}{\text {c a l / m o l e}} & \frac {\mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}}{0 . 4 0} & \frac {\left(\overline {{\mathrm {H}}} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - \mathrm {H} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {\circ}\right)}{\text {c a l / m o l e}} \\ 0. 7 0 & - 2 0 4 5 \pm 9 2 & 0. 3 3 3 & - 6 3 0 9 \pm 6 4 0 \\ 0. 6 0 & - 3 3 6 3 \pm 2 2 4 & 0. 3 0 & - 6 4 2 0 \pm 7 4 5 \\ 0. 5 0 & - 4 7 1 8 \pm 2 2 8 \end{array}
$$

Since the activity coefficient of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ is given by an analytical expression, the excess chemical potential of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ can be directly calculated from

$$
\mu_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {\mathrm {E}} = \mathrm {R T} \ln \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} \quad .
$$

Since $\ln \gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ is a function of temperature, this equation does not lend itself to easy tabular or graphic presentation (unless in a plot similar to Figure 20); however, the analytical expression should be useful for further calculations involving the excess chemical potential.

Table 6. Parameters from Correlation of Q as a Function of Temperature at Specified Compositions

# Thermodynamics of $\mathsf{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$

From the correlation of $Q$ as a function of temperature and composition for the reaction

$$
\mathrm {B e F} _ {2} (\underline {{1}}) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} (\mathrm {g}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {B e O} (\mathrm {s}) + 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g})
$$

involving pure liquid $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ , the equation expressing equilibrium constant values is

$$
\log \mathrm {K} _ {\mathrm {a}} = (3. 9 0 0 \pm 0. 0 1 9) - (4 4 1 8 \pm 1 7) (1 / \mathrm {T})
$$

or

$$
\Delta F = - (1 7. 8 5 \pm 0. 0 9) T + (2 0, 2 1 7 \pm 7 8).
$$

From this equation, the $\Delta H_{\text {reaction }}$ , and $\Delta S_{\text {reaction }}$ are 20.22 kcal/mole $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ and 17.85 eu/mole $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ , respectively. As indicated in Chapter I, the thermodynamic values of $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}(\mathsf{g})$ , $\mathsf{HF}(\mathsf{g})$ , and $\mathsf{BeO}(\mathsf{s})$ are fairly well established. Thus, by using these values and the relationships from the above reaction, new values for $\Delta F_{\mathsf{f}}^{\circ}$ , $\Delta H_{\mathsf{f}}^{\circ}$ , $\Delta S_{\mathsf{f}}^{\circ}$ , and $\mathsf{S}^{\circ}$ of $\mathsf{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ can be calculated. These are tabulated below along with the reported values from JANAF.

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">BeF2(1)</td><td rowspan="2">at</td><td>800^K</td><td>800^K</td><td>1000^K</td><td>1000^K</td></tr><tr><td>JANAF</td><td>Present Work</td><td>JANAF</td><td>Present Work</td></tr><tr><td>ΔF^0_f</td><td></td><td>-213.57</td><td>-213.60</td><td>-207.08</td><td>-208.46</td></tr><tr><td>ΔH^0_f</td><td></td><td>-240.11</td><td>-234.49</td><td>-238.92</td><td>-234.26</td></tr><tr><td>ΔS^0_f</td><td></td><td>-33.18</td><td>-26.11</td><td>-31.84</td><td>-25.80</td></tr><tr><td>S^0</td><td></td><td>30.66</td><td>37.74</td><td>35.36</td><td>41.35</td></tr></table>

While the present values are not in close agreement with the published values, they provide the most direct experimental method of evaluating the thermodynamic properties of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ . If these values for $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ are combined with the data of Greenbaum, et al. [10] for the vapor pressures of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ , new values for the thermodynamics of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\mathrm{g})$ can be obtained. These are tabulated below along with the values reported in JANAF.

<table><tr><td>BeF2(g) at ΔF0f ΔH0f ΔS0f S0</td><td>800°K JANAF -192.02 -192.29 -0.34 63.50</td><td>800°K Present Work -191.31 -181.24 1.25 76.4</td><td>1000°K JANAF -192.92 -192.63 -0.71 66.49</td><td>1000°K Present Work -193.91 -181.01 1.29 80.1</td></tr></table>

The values of $\Delta H_{\mathrm{f}}^{\circ}$ and $S^0$ of $\mathbf{BeF}_2(\mathfrak{g})$ reflect significant difference between these results and those of Greenbaum, et al. for the reaction

$$
\mathrm {B e O} (\mathrm {s}) + 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} (\mathrm {g}).
$$

Since the values of $\Delta F_{f}^{0}$ from the two methods are in reasonable agreement, the greatest source of difference must be in the temperature dependence of the equilibrium quotients.

In addition to the thermodynamic properties of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ and $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\mathrm{g})$ , the present study provides a means of determining thermodynamic properties of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\mathrm{s})$ through determination of the heat of fusion, $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ . If pure solid $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ separates upon cooling the LiF- $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ system, the relation between $\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ and the freezing temperature $\mathrm{T}$ at a given concentration $\mathrm{X}_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ in the liquid is given by

$$
d \left(\ln a _ {B e F _ {2}}\right) = d \ln \left(X _ {B e F _ {2}} \gamma_ {B e F _ {2}}\right) = \left(\Delta H _ {\text {f u s i o n}} / R T ^ {2}\right) d T.
$$

If $\Delta H_{\text{fusion}}$ is independent of $T$ , then

$$
\ln \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = - \left(\Delta \mathrm {H} _ {\text {f u s i o n}} / \mathrm {R}\right) \left(1 / \mathrm {T} - 1 / \mathrm {T} ^ {\circ}\right)
$$

with $T_{0}$ as the melting point of pure $\mathrm{BeF}_{2}$ . The values of $T$ for various compositions $X_{\mathrm{BeF}_{2}}$ were tabulated from the published phase diagram. The values of $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_{2}}$ were calculated for the specified temperatures and compositions from the derived expression given earlier. A plot of

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) \text {v s} 1 / \mathrm {T}
$$

is given in Figure 22. The slope of this correlation leads to a value

of $11 \pm 3$ kcal/mole for $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ . The other lines presented in the figure include: the line obtained if $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }} = 1.6$ kcal/mole; the line obtained if $\gamma_{\text {BeF}_2} = 1$ (i.e., log $X_{\text {BeF}_2}$ vs $1 / T$ ); the line obtained if $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }} = 7.5$ kcal/mole.

The value of 1.6 kcal/mole for $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ was included for comparison because this value was recently reported from comparison of $\Delta H_{\text {subl }}$ with $\Delta H_{\text {vap }}$ . Both measurements were from the variation of vapor pressures with temperature over solid and liquid, respectively. One discrepancy in these data, which is not mentioned in the article, is that the vapor pressures reported for the solid near the melting point are greater than the extrapolated vapor pressures of the liquid by a factor of three. The fact that the reported vapor pressures for the solid and liquid do not intersect even near the melting point is indicative of a systematic error in one or the other sets of measurements since both sets seem internally consistent.

The value of 7.5 kcal/mole for $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ was included for comparison because this is the value obtained if $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ is determined to be the difference between $\Delta H_{\text {f}}^{\circ}$ of $\text{BeF}_2(1)$ calculated here and $\Delta H_{\text {f}}^{\circ}$ of $\text{BeF}_2(s)$ reported in JANAF. $^{4}$ The $\Delta H_{\text {f}}^{\circ}$ of $\text{BeF}_2(s)$ is based on the heat of solution measurements on BeO and $\text{BeF}_2$ in aqueous HF by Kolesov, et al. $^{58}$ Since there is some question regarding the accuracy of the reported results, the value of $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ is about $7.5 \pm 6$ kcal/mole. Although the present results do not definitely establish the value of $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ of $\text{BeF}_2(s)$ , they do tend to support the value of 12 kcal/mole reported in JANAF rather than that of 2 kcal/mole used in the JANAF tabulation or the more recently reported value of 1.6 kcal/mole.

![](images/b4f3f6dff5be8d72d186fbceecc0c938be46a18dc6b6d252ae0dd276f9f7ed79.jpg)  
Fig. 22. Heat of Fusion of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ from Activities at Freezing Temperatures.

# Correlation of $\mathbf{Q}_{\mathrm{A}}$

The values of $Q_A$ reported in Tables 3 and 4 were correlated with respect to temperature and composition. Visual inspection indicated that the values exhibited linear dependence on both temperature and composition.

The general equation for the correlation was obtained in the following manner:

(1) The values at each temperature were grouped to determine the slope of the line

$$
\log Q _ {A} = (\text {s l o p e}) X _ {B e F _ {2}} + \text {c o n s t a n t}.
$$

The least-square slopes at the five experimental temperatures were as follows:

<table><tr><td>Temp (°C)</td><td>Slope</td><td>No. of Values</td></tr><tr><td>500</td><td>2.695 ± 0.473</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>550</td><td>2.480 ± 0.357</td><td>11</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>3.123 ± 0.174</td><td>22</td></tr><tr><td>650</td><td>2.847 ± 0.207</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>2.775 ± 0.175</td><td>20</td></tr></table>

(2) Using weights equal to the number of data points for each line, the average slope was determined to be 2.843. All of the values agree with this slope within the quoted standard deviations except the value at $600^{\circ}$ which is slightly larger.   
(3) With the slope of all lines set at 2.843, the value of $\log Q_{\mathrm{A}}$ at the composition intercept ( $X_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} = 0$ ) was determined at each temperature to give best fit to data as shown below:

<table><tr><td>Temp (°C)</td><td>log QA</td><td>Temp (°C)</td><td>log QA</td></tr><tr><td>500</td><td>-3.138</td><td>650</td><td>-2.757</td></tr><tr><td>550</td><td>-3.056</td><td>700</td><td>-2.638</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>-2.893</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

(4) These end values were used to calculate the function of $\log Q_{\mathrm{A}}$ vs $1 / T$ , which is $\log Q_{\mathrm{A}} = -2085(1 / T) - 0.503$ . Thus, the best smoothed values for $Q_{\mathrm{A}}$ over the experimental range are given by

$$
\log Q _ {A} = 2. 8 4 3 X _ {B e F _ {2}} - 2 0 8 5 (1 / T) - 0. 5 0 3
$$

The observed values of $Q_{A}$ and the calculated values based on the above function are shown in Figure 23. Since the data at the various temperatures overlap, the coordinates are specified for each line independently. The standard deviations of the points are not shown in the figure, but most points agree within the quoted standard deviations in Table 3. The values determined in BeO-saturated melts are shown by closed symbols and those in unsaturated melts by open symbols.

The concentration of hydroxide present at equilibrium for known partial pressures of HF and $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ above a melt of known composition could be calculated using the relationship for $Q_{A}$ ,

$$
Q _ {A} = \left(P _ {H F} / P _ {H 2 O}\right) [ O H ^ {-} ] = m o l e / k g,
$$

derived from the reaction

$$
\mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {F} ^ {-} (\text {s o l n}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} (\text {s o l n})
$$

Other equilibria involving hydroxide were also introduced in Chapter I. One reaction is

$$
\mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} (\mathrm {s o l n}) \rightleftharpoons 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} (\mathrm {s o l n})
$$

from which

$$
Q _ {B} = \left[ O H ^ {-} \right] ^ {2} / P _ {H _ {2} O} = \left(Q _ {A}\right) ^ {2} / Q
$$

for a BeO-saturated melt. Since expressions for both Q and $Q_{A}$ are known, the values of $Q_{B}$ can be derived. If the equation given earlier for $\log (Q / X_{\mathrm{BeF}_2})$ is rearranged in terms of $X_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ rather than $X_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ , the following equation is obtained

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = - 0. 9 4 0 - 4 5 9 6 (1 / \mathrm {T}) + [ 3 5. 0 0 - 1 0 1 6 8 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {X} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} \\ + [ - 6 8. 1 4 + 2 6 1 3 2 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {2} + [ 5 0. 6 4 - 2 1 0 4 8 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {3} \\ + [ - 1 2. 6 6 + 5 2 6 2 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] X _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {4} \\ \end{array}
$$

If this equation is combined with the expression for $\mathbb{Q}_{\mathbf{A}}$ , the following holds:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log Q _ {B} = 2 \log Q _ {A} - \log Q = - 0. 0 6 6 + 4 2 6 (1 / T) + [ - 2 9. 3 l + 1 0 1 6 8 (1 / T) ] x _ {B e F _ {2}} \\ + [ 6 8. 1 4 - 2 6 1 3 2 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {x} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {2} + [ - 5 0. 6 4 + 2 1 0 4 8 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {x} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {3} \\ + [ 1 2. 6 6 - 5 2 6 2 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] X _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {4} - \log X _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} \\ \end{array}
$$

The parameters of the equation

$$
\log Q _ {B} = \text {s l o p e} (1 / T) + \text {c o n s t a n t}
$$

are tabulated below for various experimental compositions:

<table><tr><td>\( \frac{\mathrm{X}}{\mathrm{{BeF}}2} \)</td><td>Slope</td><td>Constant</td><td>\( \frac{\mathrm{X}}{\mathrm{{BeF}}2} \)</td><td>Slope</td><td>Constant</td></tr><tr><td>0.30</td><td>1649</td><td>-3.367</td><td>0.60</td><td>983</td><td>-2.197</td></tr><tr><td>0.333</td><td>1629</td><td>-3.513</td><td>0.70</td><td>695</td><td>-1.370</td></tr><tr><td>0.40</td><td>1524</td><td>-3.415</td><td>0.80</td><td>456</td><td>-0.571</td></tr><tr><td>0.50</td><td>1279</td><td>-2.925</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

Another reaction involving hydroxide is

$$
\mathrm {H F} (g) + \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} (\mathrm {s o l n}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} (\mathrm {s o l n}) + \mathrm {F} ^ {-} (\mathrm {s o l n})
$$

from which

$$
Q _ {C} = \left[ O H ^ {-} \right] / P _ {H F} = Q _ {A} / Q \text {f o r a B e 0 - s a t u r a t e d m e l t}.
$$

Therefore,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log Q _ {C} = \log Q _ {A} - \log Q = 0. 4 3 7 + 2 5 1 1 (1 / T) + [ - 3 2. 1 6 + 1 0 1 6 8 (1 / T) ] X _ {B e F _ {2}} \\ + [ 6 8. 1 4 - 2 6 1 3 2 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {2} + [ - 5 0. 6 4 + 2 1 0 4 8 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {3} \\ + [ 1 2. 6 6 - 5 2 6 2 (1 / \mathrm {T}) ] \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} ^ {4} - \log \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}} \\ \end{array}
$$

The parameters of the equation

$$
\log Q _ {C} = \text {s l o p e (l / T)} + \text {c o n s t a n t}
$$

are tabulated below for various experimental compositions:

<table><tr><td>\( X_{BeF_2} \)</td><td>Slope</td><td>Constant</td><td>\( X_{BeF_2} \)</td><td>Slope</td><td>Constant</td></tr><tr><td>0.30</td><td>3734</td><td>-3.819</td><td>0.60</td><td>3068</td><td>-3.404</td></tr><tr><td>0.333</td><td>3714</td><td>-3.960</td><td>0.70</td><td>2780</td><td>-2.862</td></tr><tr><td>0.40</td><td>3609</td><td>-4.052</td><td>0.80</td><td>2541</td><td>-2.348</td></tr><tr><td>0.50</td><td>3364</td><td>-3.847</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

If the above functions are used to evaluate equilibrium quotients for 0.333 BeF $_2$ , the values obtained and $\Delta H_{\mathrm{r}}$ based on integration of the van't Hoff equation are given below:

<table><tr><td>Equilibrium 
Quotients</td><td>Q at 800°C</td><td>Q at 1000°C</td><td>ΔHc(kcal/mole)</td></tr><tr><td>QA</td><td>6.90 x 10-3</td><td>2.29 x 10-2</td><td>9.5</td></tr><tr><td>QB</td><td>3.33 x 10-2</td><td>1.30 x 10-2</td><td>-7.5</td></tr><tr><td>QC</td><td>4.80</td><td>5.70 x 10-1</td><td>-17.0</td></tr></table>

Each of these equilibrium quotients is in terms of moles of hydroxide per kilogram of melt due to experimental expediency, but they could be converted to the mole fraction scale by dividing each by moles of solvent per kilogram of melt. This conversion would not affect the $\Delta H_{\mathrm{r}}$ values. The above values of equilibrium quotients involving hydroxide may not provide enough information to establish the complete nature of hydroxide in the LiF-BeF $_2$ system,

but they do allow quantitative prediction of the extent of conversion of oxide to hydroxide, which is the principal controlling step in the removal of oxide by HF-sparging.

![](images/74591d237b99aff75f2c99d907906e3122c17f7f11505a779c35286f3e314932.jpg)  
Fig. 23. Correlation of $\log Q_{\mathrm{A}}$ as a Function of Composition and Temperature.

# Correlation of $Q_{0}$

The values of $Q_0$ reported in Table 4 were correlated as a function of temperature at each composition. The parameters of the equation $\log Q_0 = \text{slope}(1 / T) + \text{constant}$ are given below:

<table><tr><td>XBeF2</td><td>slope ± σ</td><td>constant ± σ</td><td>No. of Points</td></tr><tr><td>0.273</td><td>-8158 ± 500</td><td>4.791 ± 0.549</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>0.333</td><td>-8637 ± 349</td><td>5.673 ± 0.401</td><td>24</td></tr><tr><td>0.400</td><td>-9032 ± 706</td><td>7.026 ± 0.812</td><td>4</td></tr></table>

The values of $Q_0$ and the corresponding correlations as a function of temperature are shown in Figure 24. Since the two values for 0.60 BeF $_2$ would not yield a line with slope or intercept similar to the others, a line was drawn parallel to the line at 0.40 BeF $_2$ so as to be equidistant between the two values at 0.60 BeF $_2$ .

Measurement of $Q_{0}$ at high $BeF_{2}$ compositions is limited because the rate of change in $P_{HF}$ and $P_{H_{2}O}$ with respect to $W$ approaches the precision of measurements of the low $P_{H_{2}O}$ and high $P_{HF}$ encountered. Although additional experiments were performed on 0.60 $BeF_{2}$ , convergence in the solution of equations to obtain $Q_{0}, Q_{A}, r_{o}$ , and $s_{o}$ was not reached due to the experimental limitation mentioned above.

The data on unsaturated melts were neither extensive nor precise enough to permit a meaningful correlation with respect to melt composition. However, the solubility of BeO could be determined by comparison of these measurements with those of BeO-saturated melts.

As described in Chapter I,

$$
Q _ {O} = \left(P _ {H F}\right) ^ {2} \left[ O ^ {2 -} \right] / P _ {H _ {2} O}
$$

and since for a BeO-saturated melt

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(\mathrm {P} _ {\mathrm {H F}}\right) ^ {2} / \mathrm {P} _ {\mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}} = 0, \\ \left[ 0 ^ {2 ^ {-}} \right] _ {\text {s a t}} = Q _ {0} / Q. \\ \end{array}
$$

The value of $Q$ at BeO saturation was established in the earlier work. Division of $Q_{0}$ by $Q$ for the same melt composition and temperature would provide the concentration of oxide at saturation.

The values of $Q$ needed to determine BeO solubility are given by the following parameters for the equation

$\log Q = \text{slope}(1 / T) + \text{constant}$ :

<table><tr><td>\( X_{BeF_2} \)</td><td>Slope</td><td>Constant</td></tr><tr><td>0.273</td><td>-5900</td><td>4.150</td></tr><tr><td>0.333</td><td>-5674</td><td>4.233</td></tr><tr><td>0.400</td><td>-5565</td><td>4.528</td></tr><tr><td>0.600</td><td>-5274</td><td>4.745</td></tr></table>

The solubilities in moles per kilogram of oxide in the various experiments, as determined from $\mathbb{Q}_0 / \mathbb{Q}$ using observed $\mathbb{Q}_0$ values and smoothed $\mathbb{Q}$ values from the above relation, are given in Table 8. Since

$$
[ o ^ {2 -} ] = Q _ {0} / Q,
$$

$\log [0^{2^{-}}] = \log Q_{0} - \log Q$

and as both $\log Q_0$ and $\log Q$ show linear dependence with $(1 / T)$ , $\log [0^{2^{-}}]$ should also show linear dependence. Combination of the parameters for $Q_0$ and $Q$ leads to

$\log [0^{2^{-}}] = \mathrm{slope}(1 / \mathrm{T}) + \mathrm{constant}$

<table><tr><td>\( X_{BeF_2} \)</td><td>Slope</td><td>Constant</td></tr><tr><td>0.273</td><td>-2258</td><td>0.641</td></tr><tr><td>0.333</td><td>-2963</td><td>1.440</td></tr><tr><td>0.400</td><td>-3467</td><td>2.498</td></tr></table>

Each of the observed solubilities is shown in Figure 25 along with the calculated expressions for the three compositions tabulated above.

Since many more experiments were performed on $0.333\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , the range of measured solubilities at each temperature provides a rough measure of just how well these are known. A clear trend of solubility with temperature is shown:

$$
\begin{array}{l} 5 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C} \quad (2. 5 - 6. 0) \times 1 0 ^ {- 3} \text {m o l e s / k g} \\ 6 0 0 \quad (7. 2 - 1 1. 8) \times 1 0 ^ {- 3} \\ 7 0 0 \quad (2. 3 - 3. 8) \quad x 1 0 ^ {- 2} \\ \end{array}
$$

The other values indicate that $\mathrm{BeO}$ solubility may be increasing slightly with $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , but the increase is not very great.

Fig. 25. Solubility of BeO as a Function of Temperature for Various Melt Compositions.

# Summary

These studies of equilibria between HF, $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}$ , and molten LiF-BeF $_2$ had a twofold purpose: (1) to determine as much as possible about the thermodynamics of the LiF-BeF $_2$ system, and (2) to determine as much as possible about the oxide chemistry of the system.

The thermodynamic activities of LiF and $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ were determined as a function of temperature and composition. The major characteristics of the system include:

(1) Positive deviations from ideality at high $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ concentrations.   
(2) Increasing positive deviations of the activity with temperature indicating the probability of a miscibility gap near $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .   
(3) Combination of activities with previously published phase diagram indicate a higher value for $\Delta H_{\text {fusion }}$ of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ than currently used in thermodynamic compilations.   
(4) Activities determined with BeO present are in agreement with limited previous data.

Extrapolation of data to pure liquid $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , which is inaccessible experimentally because of high viscosity, provides a means of determining thermodynamic properties of $\mathrm{BeF}_2(\underline{1})$ .

The equilibria between $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , HF, and dissolved hydroxide were studied by estimating, from material balance calculations on the gas phase, the amount of OH formed in or removed from the melt upon reaction. The results from measurements on both BeO-saturated and unsaturated melts were consistent. The results indicate that significant amounts of hydroxide were formed in the presence of O.Ol atm HF, but OH could not exist in the absence of HF and $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ .

The equilibria between $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , HF, and dissolved oxide were also evaluated with melts not saturated with BeO. The solubility of BeO was estimated by combination of these results with those for BeO-saturated melts.

These results suggest methods to attack at least two allied problems:

(1) Determination of thermodynamic activities of other fluorides dissolved in the LiF-BeF $_2$ system; and   
(2) Determination of the amount of oxide in fluoride melts.

The procedure used here to determine activities of BeO-saturated melts could be applied to melts saturated with other sparingly soluble oxides. Variation in Q with amount of corresponding fluoride dissolved in melt would provide information necessary to calculate change in activities.

The experiments performed here on unsaturated melts required determination of the concentration of oxide present at the start of an experiment in addition to the equilibrium quotients. If oxide analyses were required on similar melts, the equilibrium quotients could be evaluated and treated as fixed parameters, thus the variation in effluent $\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{HF}}$ and $\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}}$ to a small value of W (about 1.0) could be used to calculate the amount of oxide in the sample prior to treatment. In principle, this should work no matter whether oxide were being added or removed. The presence of saturating solid oxide would complicate the analysis, but the total amount of oxide present could be determined by treatment with anhydrous HF and measurement of the evolved $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}$ .

# APPENDIX A

# Partial Pressures from Studies on BeO-Saturated Melts

The experiments are arranged according to a three digit "Run No." which indicates chronological order. There are three series (100, 200, 400) included. A brief history of each series is given in Chapter III.

The information provided for each experiment includes:

Run No.

Composition of melt (expressed as mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ )

Temperature of melt, $^\circ \mathrm{C}$

w = weight of melt, kg

T = temperature of wet-test meter, ${}^{\mathrm{o}}\mathrm{K}$

a = influent $\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{HF}}$ atm x 103

c = influent P H₂O, atm x 10³

The effluent partial pressures, $\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{HF}} = \mathsf{x}$ and $\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{H_2O}} = \mathsf{y}$ , are tabulated (atm x 10³) with the corresponding initial and final values of W (V/wRT). Note that the use of W allows consistent comparison of experiments independent of the weight of melt or temperature of gas-measurement. The equilibrium quotients evaluated from each experiment are presented in Table 3.

# APPENDIX B

Partial Pressures from Studies on Unsaturated (with respect to Be0) Melts

The experiments are arranged according to a three digit "Run No." which indicates chronological order. There are three series (300, 500, 600) included. A brief history of each series is given in Chapter III.

The information provided for each experiment includes: Run No.

Composition of melt (expressed as mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ )

Temperature of melt, $^\circ \mathrm{C}$

w = weight of melt, kg

T = temperature of wet-test meter, $^\circ \mathrm{K}$

a + bW = influent $\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{HF}}$ atm x 10³

c + dW = influent $\mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}}$ atm x 103

The effluent partial pressures, $P_{\mathrm{HF}} = x$ and $P_{\mathrm{H_2O}} = y$ , are tabulated (atm $x10^3$ ) with the corresponding initial and final values of W. Note that the use of W allows consistent comparison of experiments independent of the weight of melt or temperature of gas-measurement. The equilibrium quotients evaluated from each experiment are presented in Table 4.

Run No. 301

# APPENDIX C

# Glossary

a (or a + bW) - influent partial pressure of HF

A - (a + 2c)

aBeF2 thermodynamic activity of BeF2

$\alpha, \beta$ - parameters expressing the variation in $\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ with composition at a specified temperature according to the equation

$$
\log \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = c X ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + \beta X ^ {4} _ {\mathrm {L i F}}
$$

c (or c + dW) - influent partial pressure of H2O

C - integration constants

$\triangle \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{p}}$ - heat capacity change at constant pressure

f - correction factor = $\frac{P_w - (x + y)}{P_w - P_w}$

$\Delta \mathbf{F}_{\mathbf{r}}$ - free energy of reaction

$\gamma_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ - activity coefficient of $\mathrm{BeF}_2 = a_{\mathrm{BeF}_2} / x_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$

$\Delta \mathbf{H}_{\mathrm{f}}^{\circ}$ standard heat of formation

$\Delta \mathrm{H}_{\text {fusion }}$ - heat of fusion

$\Delta H_{r}$ - heat of reaction

$\Delta \mathrm{H}_{\mathrm{sub}}$ - heat of sublimation

$\Delta \mathrm{H}_{\mathrm{vap}}$ - heat of vaporization

$\overline{\mathbb{H}}_{\mathsf{BeF}_2}$ - partial molal heat content of $\mathsf{BeF_2}$ in solution

$\mathbf{H}_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}^0$ - partial molal heat content of pure liquid $\mathbf{BeF}_2$

K - thermodynamic equilibrium constant

$$
K _ {a} - X ^ {2} / y a _ {B e F _ {2}} = Q / a _ {B e F _ {2}}
$$

k, l, m - parameters used in correlation of Q as a function of melt composition according to the equation

$$
\log \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {X} _ {\text {B e F} _ {2}}\right) = \mathrm {k} + 1 \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\text {L i F}}\right) ^ {2} + \mathrm {m} \left(\mathrm {X} _ {\text {L i F}}\right) ^ {4}
$$

$\mathbf{k}^{\circ}, \mathbf{l}^{\circ}, \mathbf{m}^{\circ}$ - temperature independent portions of $\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{l}$ , and $\mathbf{m}$ , respectively

$\mathbf{k}^{\prime}, \mathbf{l}^{\prime}, \mathbf{m}^{\prime}$ - temperature dependent portions of $\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{l}$ , and $\mathbf{m}$ , respectively

nHF and nH2O - moles of HF and H2O as measured at T, Pw

$\mathbb{P}_{\mathrm{HF}}$ and $\mathbb{P}_{\mathrm{H_2O}}$ - partial pressures of HF and $\mathbf{H}_2\mathbf{O}$ , respectively.

$\mathbf{p}_{\mathbf{w}}$ vapor pressure of water at $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{w}}$

$\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{W}}$ - total pressure at wet-test meter

P1 - partial pressure of HF or $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ leaving melt

P2 - measured partial pressure of HF or $\mathbf{H}_2\mathbf{O}$

Q - $x^{2} / y$ for BeO saturated melt

$$
Q _ {A} = x s / y
$$

$$
Q _ {b} - s ^ {2} / y r = \left(Q _ {A}\right) ^ {2} / Q _ {O}
$$

$$
Q _ {B} - s ^ {2} / y = \left(Q _ {A}\right) ^ {2} / Q
$$

$$
Q _ {c} - s / x r = Q _ {A} / Q _ {O}
$$

$$
Q _ {C} - s / x = Q _ {A} / Q
$$

$$
Q _ {0} - x ^ {2} r / y
$$

r - [O²⁻] - oxide concentration in moles per kilogram of melt

s - [OH- ] - hydroxide concentration in moles per kilogram of melt

$r_o$ and $s_o$ - values of $r$ and $s$ at $W = 0$ in unsaturated experiments

$\mathbf{S}^{\circ}$ - standard entropy at specified temperature

$\Delta S_{r}$ entropy of reaction

$\sigma$ - standard deviation

t - degrees Centigrade (generally used to indicate melt temperatures)

T - degrees Kelvin (used to indicate wet-test meter temperatures)

$\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{W}}$ - temperature of wet-test meter, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{K}$ $\mu_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}^{\mathrm{E}}$ - excess chemical potential of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ $\mathbf{V}_{\mathbf{a}}$ - volume of dry carrier gas going through system measured at $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{w}}, \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{w}}$ $\mathbf{V}_{\mathbf{b}}$ - volume of gas entering titration assembly at $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{w}}, \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{w}}$ $\mathbf{V}_{\mathbf{m}}$ - volume of gas space above the melt $\mathbf{V}_{\mathbf{w}}$ - volume of gas through melt measured at $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{w}}, \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{w}}$ (liters)  
dV - increment of gas flowing through system as measured at $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{w}}, \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{w}}$ w - weight of melt ( $\mathbf{kg}$ )  
W - V/wRT $_{\mathbf{w}}$ (mole $\mathbf{kg}^{-1}$ atm $^{-1}$ ) $\mathbf{X}_{\mathbf{BeF}_2}$ - mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ x - effluent partial pressure of HF  
y - effluent partial pressure of $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$

#
